<div id="review_1392949935" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1392949935" />
    <a title="Ms.pegasus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5015851-ms-pegasus"><img alt="Ms.pegasus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335705124p2/5015851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1392949935?book_show_action=true">Sep 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ms.pegasus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ms.pegasus" href="/user/show/5015851-ms-pegasus">Ms.pegasus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/15997610-flashman">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone interested in history</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Ms.pegasus by: </span>
      my husband
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5015851-ms-pegasus?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5015851-ms-pegasus?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1392949935" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3645888716654783659">A braggart, bully, liar, cheat, lecher, racist, cad, wastrel and coward — how does author George MacDonald Fraser make a riveting and even sympathetic character out of such material? He pulls it off with deceptive ease. First, there is the backdrop of Victorian hypocrisy. Behind a public show of prudery and self-satisfied morality flourished a robust traffic in prostitution and indifference to social injustice. Abroad, diplomats cynically formed duplicitous alliances while jockeying for power an</span>
  <span id="freeText3645888716654783659" style="display:none">A braggart, bully, liar, cheat, lecher, racist, cad, wastrel and coward — how does author George MacDonald Fraser make a riveting and even sympathetic character out of such material? He pulls it off with deceptive ease. First, there is the backdrop of Victorian hypocrisy. Behind a public show of prudery and self-satisfied morality flourished a robust traffic in prostitution and indifference to social injustice. Abroad, diplomats cynically formed duplicitous alliances while jockeying for power and pursuing a policy of destabilizing local regimes. A caste of blue-bloods promoted incompetence in government. In the military the combination of class preferment and the practice of purchasing commissions produced some of the greatest military debacles in history. Finally, Flashman labors toward the modest and quite modern aspiration of self-preservation. The lengths his “betters” go to endanger him in the name of Victorian brio create a series of humorous predicaments. The reader experiences a certain satisfaction seeing Flashman's quick wit and pragmatism emerge time after time triumphant.<br /><br />This is the first of the Flashman books. The 80 year old Flashman, having been showered with medals and honors is writing his memoirs, — confiding to the reader the true history of his time. That, at least, is the conceit of the books. Fraser resurrects the bully in Thomas Hughes' TOM BROWN'S SCHOOL DAYS. The school is Rugby, ruled by headmaster Thomas Arnold, rod in one hand and Bible in the other. (Although Arnold had passed on by then, this was the same Rugby that Charles Dodgson attended and forever looked back on as a formative trauma of childhood). Flashman is expelled for, of all things, drunkeness. While Arnold upbraids him with sanctimonious reproaches, Flashman reflects: <i>“...he was a hypocrite I think, like most of his generation. Either that or he was more foolish than he looked, for he was wasting his piety on me.”</i><br /><br />Expelled from Rugby, Flashman persuades his father to purchase his commission in a regiment just returned from India, and therefore not likely to be posted to active duty any time soon. The commander is Lord Cardigan whom Fraser has animated with a speech impediment reminiscent of Elmer Fudd. Cardigan, of course, is famed for leading the charge of the light brigade. This is still well before that fateful debacle. Flashman offers his assessment:<i> “He was God's own original fool, there's no doubt about that — although he was not to blame for the fiasco at Balaclava; that was Raglan and Airey between them....He was just stupid, too stupid ever to be afraid....For all that, he could never be called a bad soldier. Some human faults are military virtues, like stupidity, and arrogance, and narrow-mindedness....”</i><br /><br />Fraser is at his best when he mingles thrilling fictional episodes with real historic events. For this reason, the early sections of the book felt a bit slow to me. The writing becomes vivid when Flashman is plucked from his cozy billeting and tumbles into Afghanistan. Historical authenticity is enhanced by Fraser's use of footnotes citing authoritative sources to argue controversial points in Flashman's narrative. Fraser brings history to life. Rather than feeling bewildered by the historical details, my interest was piqued. I eagerly consulted online sources for more details about the first Anglo-Afghan War, an event of which I was totally ignorant. Flashman introduces the military commander Lord Elphinstone at the center of this disaster as follows: <i>“Elphy ably assisted by McNaghten, was about to reach the peak of his career; he was going to produce the most shameful, ridiculous disaster in British military history.”</i> That event in 1842 was the annihilation of some 16,000 British soldiers, family members and camp followers in an attempted retreat from Kabul. <br /><br />I highly recommend these books to anyone interested in a combination of history and great writing. <br /><br />NOTES:<br />Peter Hitchens writes an excellent commentary on the series in his blog: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://hitchensblog.mailonsunday.co.uk/2013/10/flashman-revisited-the-genius-of-george-macdonald-fraser.html">http://hitchensblog.mailonsunday.co.u...</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3645888716654783659" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1262118989" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1262118989" />
    <a title="S.J. Arnott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41664094-s-j-arnott"><img alt="S.J. Arnott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1427622373p2/41664094.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1262118989?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="S.J. Arnott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="S.J. Arnott" href="/user/show/41664094-s-j-arnott">S.J. Arnott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/13247885-flashman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41664094-s-j-arnott?shelf=flashman">flashman</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1262118989" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13215930176244574642">Poor old Flash.<br/><br/>What would he say to all these one star reviewers? Actually, he’d probably damn their eyes, then nip round the corner to pay a couple of toughs to knock them down, de-bag them and blacken their backsides with boot-polish...<br/><br/>I can see why some people might take against Flash after reading the first book in the series. Although I think it’s excellent, it has slightly rougher edges than the ones that follow and portrays our protagonist more as the vicious schoolboy he was, rather than</span>
  <span id="freeText13215930176244574642" style="display:none">Poor old Flash.<br /><br />What would he say to all these one star reviewers? Actually, he’d probably damn their eyes, then nip round the corner to pay a couple of toughs to knock them down, de-bag them and blacken their backsides with boot-polish...<br /><br />I can see why some people might take against Flash after reading the first book in the series. Although I think it’s excellent, it has slightly rougher edges than the ones that follow and portrays our protagonist more as the vicious schoolboy he was, rather than the reluctant pseudo-hero he becomes. His less than gallant treatment of his <i>de facto</i> step-mother is probably the best example of this, which is something of a shock I’ll admit, but I have less sympathy with those who make accusations of racism, most of which appear to be based solely on the language used in the book. These criticisms miss the point that this is exactly the type of language that would have been used at the time, and it wouldn’t have raised an eyebrow in most company. This unabashed attention to detail is one of the author’s great strengths. Too many historical novels feature characters who have the sensibilities of 20th-century liberal arts teachers, whereas Fraser really gets under the skin of Flashman, the archetypal Victorian scoundrel.<br /><br />So please don’t let overly-sensitive reviews put you off <i>Flashman</i>. This first book sets up Flash’s dilemma for the rest of the series; namely, that once he’s mistaken for a dashing hero, various ghastly people (Prime Ministers, generals, unspeakable foreigners, and even members of the Royal family) insist on sending him to dangerous places (mostly hot and dusty) to do horribly dangerous heroic things, while all Flash wants is a peaceful life, dividing his time between fashionable London society and the fleshpots of the East End.<br /><br />Unfortunately, the next book <i> Royal Flash</i> is one of the weaker in the series (being little more of a reworking of <i>The Prisoner of Zenda</i>) but after that, it’s all gravy.<br /><br />(Note on covers. I love the original Arthur Barbosa book covers. I think they capture the essence of Flashman perfectly: handsome, manly, fresh-faced, so obviously trustworthy (!). In contrast, the more recent covers offer us a boozy, sly, caricature of a cad that completely miss the mark: Flashman doesn’t proper because he looks like a pantomime villain; he prospers because he doesn’t.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="13215930176244574642" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_124411938" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124411938" />
    <a title="Noble" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4366761-noble"><img alt="Noble" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/124411938?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Noble" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Noble" href="/user/show/4366761-noble">Noble</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780452259614" href="/book/show/401880.Flashman">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer124411938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4931427985839626817">I'm going to review the whole series here, instead of saying more or less the same thing for each of the books I've read.<br/><br/>Flashman, the character, is an asshole. Really, he doesn't have many redeeming qualities. I shall list those which come to mind:<br/>He seems to genuinely love his wife, in his own way (he cheats on her, or tries to, pretty much constantly, but he also has ample reason to believe she does the same and knows about his own infidelities; still, his thoughts seem to go back to her more</span>
  <span id="freeText4931427985839626817" style="display:none">I'm going to review the whole series here, instead of saying more or less the same thing for each of the books I've read.<br /><br />Flashman, the character, is an asshole. Really, he doesn't have many redeeming qualities. I shall list those which come to mind:<br />He seems to genuinely love his wife, in his own way (he cheats on her, or tries to, pretty much constantly, but he also has ample reason to believe she does the same and knows about his own infidelities; still, his thoughts seem to go back to her more often than anything else, aside from saving his own skin at least).<br />He generally doesn't take joy in meaningless slaughter (if he does take joy in someone dying, they're either also a total asshole or he's just glad he's not the one being killed).<br />He seems to have more respect for people of color than most people in the setting (he uses the dreaded "N word" a lot, but it's mostly set in the 1800s, so that doesn't mean much; mind you, he doesn't respect anyone all that much, but minorities don't get any more of his disrespect or contempt than anyone else, really - a caveat here is that he usually doesn't have a problem with slavery, but that goes back to his simply not caring about most people other than himself).<br />That's it. He's a coward, a womanizer, a thief, and a liar. He will readily abandon his allies at the drop of a hat, flee a battle, and revel in the glory afforded him when he is the sole survivor (even if he survived only because he shoved a "friend" into the line of fire and dove into the latrine when the battle started). He's a cad.<br /><br />That said, he's a cynical bastard whose reflections on how scewed up the world he lived in really was are bloody hilarious. The fact that he so often speaks plainly of his many shortcomings helps.<br /><br />It is also worth nothing that Fraser did his homework, and includes many people and events that are really rather interesting in their own right (if Flashy mentions a general or monarch, or battle, or artifact, you can spend hours just doing a Wiki-crawl learning about the craziness inherent in all of it).</span>
  <a data-text-id="4931427985839626817" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_549841741" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/549841741" />
    <a title="Kirsten Mortensen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6695939-kirsten-mortensen"><img alt="Kirsten Mortensen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598536959p2/6695939.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/549841741?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirsten Mortensen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirsten Mortensen" href="/user/show/6695939-kirsten-mortensen">Kirsten Mortensen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/13247885-flashman">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer549841741" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9473515932771341051">Okay, I'll admit it. Perhaps because I'm a gal, a couple chapters into this book I wondered if I was going to have to force myself to keep going.<br/><br/>Because -- as you know if you know anything about the Flashman series -- the narrator of these books is *not* a nice guy.<br/><br/>But something happened as I reached a quarter mark or so of the novel: I found myself hooked. <br/><br/>And by the time Flashy found himself in Afghanistan (the series' conceit is that Flashman was an eyewitness to a number of significant 19th </span>
  <span id="freeText9473515932771341051" style="display:none">Okay, I'll admit it. Perhaps because I'm a gal, a couple chapters into this book I wondered if I was going to have to force myself to keep going.<br /><br />Because -- as you know if you know anything about the Flashman series -- the narrator of these books is *not* a nice guy.<br /><br />But something happened as I reached a quarter mark or so of the novel: I found myself hooked. <br /><br />And by the time Flashy found himself in Afghanistan (the series' conceit is that Flashman was an eyewitness to a number of significant 19th century military events, including in this book the British army's 1842 Retreat from Kabul, <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1842_retreat_from_Kabul">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1842_ret...</a>) my opinion of him had shifted. Yes, he's a despicable man: a coward, a misogynist, a liar. But all the same, I was rooting for him. I wanted him to somehow get out alive . . . <br /><br />You couldn't ask for a better example of how to make an unappealing character sympathetic, in fact. <br /><br />One last point. Someone in a Goodreads group thread (who hasn't read the book) speculated that Flashman might be considered "post-modern" -- a reasonable assumption given that the narrator is an anti-hero. But to my eye, the book transcends any neat categories. Yes, it can be read as a blistering critique of 19th century British imperialism, but it's dealing with historical facts -- a refreshing change from contemporary polemics that are out to score political points at the expense of everything else. <br /><br />And while the book (consistent with post-modernist writing) eschews any pretense to moral elevation, technically the writing and structure is both original and virtually flawless. So there's plenty of aesthetic pleasure to be had in the reading. <br /><br />I've also seen Flashman called a historical novel. I suppose that's as good a category as any. But there's something not quite right about that characterization, either. Or maybe I think of historical novels as more atmospheric. Flashman is set in the mid-1900s, but it reads like a contemporary. <br /><br />Recommend to anyone who likes terrific story-telling &amp; can overlook very un-P.C. characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9473515932771341051" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17292640038993110137" style="display:none">Cowardice is a virtue. Just when it seems he's about to exhibit a noble emotion - love - he is cuckolded; an indignity he happily accepts to keep the money flowing. Top hole!</span>

<div id="review_978391658" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/978391658" />
    <a title="Victor Bruneski" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29425183-victor-bruneski"><img alt="Victor Bruneski" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442987549p2/29425183.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/978391658?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Victor Bruneski" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Victor Bruneski" href="/user/show/29425183-victor-bruneski">Victor Bruneski</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330024846" href="/book/show/1690817.Flashman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29425183-victor-bruneski?shelf=my-top-ten-books-series">my-top-ten-books-series</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer978391658" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3305711533593237988">I picked this book up after a mere reference to Flashman in The Bookman Histories. From what I read in the reviews it looks like just my cup of tea, and boy howdy is it.<br/><br/>The novel is told by Harry Flashman, the same Harry Flashman who's claim of fame is from <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1496303.Tom_Brown_s_Schooldays___Tom_Brown_at_Oxford' title='Tom Brown's Schooldays & Tom Brown at Oxford by Thomas Hughes' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tom Brown's Schooldays & Tom Brown at Oxford</a> (I had no idea of that book before this). The author decided to write some further adventures of the school bully, to hilarious results.<br/><br/>The story starts out right after Harry Flashman is kicked out</span>
  <span id="freeText3305711533593237988" style="display:none">I picked this book up after a mere reference to Flashman in The Bookman Histories. From what I read in the reviews it looks like just my cup of tea, and boy howdy is it.<br /><br />The novel is told by Harry Flashman, the same Harry Flashman who's claim of fame is from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1496303.Tom_Brown_s_Schooldays___Tom_Brown_at_Oxford" title="Tom Brown's Schooldays &amp; Tom Brown at Oxford by Thomas Hughes" rel="nofollow noopener">Tom Brown's Schooldays &amp; Tom Brown at Oxford</a> (I had no idea of that book before this). The author decided to write some further adventures of the school bully, to hilarious results.<br /><br />The story starts out right after Harry Flashman is kicked out of school, the last you hear of him in School Days. You will notice right away Harry Flashman is unlike most protagonists you are use to, even the scoundrel like. He's a bully, a coward, a liar and a racist (although most were back then so maybe I shouldn't count that). He's not above hitting woman and even once going so far as raping one. In summary, he is a complete ASSHOLE. But in the end you can't help but like him.<br /><br />He makes bad decision after bad decision, eventually ending up in Afghanistan. Back then it was occupied for a foreign force who was barely holding on to it, like now. Where his bad decision land him in hot water, his incredible good luck gets him out of it, and even ahead.<br /><br />Not only is is one of the funniest novels I have ever read, but also one of the most thoroughly researched. It is quite amazing that Fraser is able to add all of that historical detail, and keep the story funny and entertaining. I can't wait to read the next part, Royal Flush.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3305711533593237988" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1759968753" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1759968753" />
    <a title="N.N. Light" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23234551-n-n-light"><img alt="N.N. Light" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483558201p2/23234551.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1759968753?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2017</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_728242505" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/728242505" />
    <a title="Nate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6803829-nate"><img alt="Nate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413944642p2/6803829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/728242505?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate" href="/user/show/6803829-nate">Nate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780452259614" href="/book/show/401880.Flashman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer728242505" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7250822864622482377">It's rare for me to actually feel guilty for enjoying the antics of a protagonist but I had this nagging cognitive dissonance the entire time I was reading this--Harry Flashman is certainly one of the more repulsive, boorish fictional people I've rooted for. He's devoid of pretty much every trait humans find admirable and stuffed with every trait we find deplorable...I'm struggling to think of one positive thing about him. And yet, I can't deny there was a part of me that took a perverted glee i</span>
  <span id="freeText7250822864622482377" style="display:none">It's rare for me to actually feel guilty for enjoying the antics of a protagonist but I had this nagging cognitive dissonance the entire time I was reading this--Harry Flashman is certainly one of the more repulsive, boorish fictional people I've rooted for. He's devoid of pretty much every trait humans find admirable and stuffed with every trait we find deplorable...I'm struggling to think of one positive thing about him. And yet, I can't deny there was a part of me that took a perverted glee in watching him be such an asshole. If he's not outright being a dick to someone, he's mentally savaging them while wearing a disingenuous smile. I mean, this is seriously inspired douchebaggery. It's fascinating as to why people, including myself, enjoy seeing him succeed at being such a cock..it's probably because Harry is both charismatic and even clever in his own way. There's tons of lines in this book that made me chuckle at the pure brazen meanness.<br /><br />The fascinating title character aside, this was a fun story. It concerns Flashman's joining the army and his involvement in the first Anglo-Afghan war, specifically the infamous British retreat from Kabul in which sixteen to seventeen thousand people were slaughtered or enslaved (the whole of the retreating force). One of Fraser's strengths is that he's obviously done the research and brought it to life in a real way. Even though this is a funny book the people and details are treated seriously, which I loved. There's about thirty footnotes scattered about the story that provide more detail on the history. Many of the real people involved are here as main characters, most notably the leading British general William Elphinstone. Man, Fraser apparently thought very little about Elphinstone because some of the most scathing comments in the book are directed at him. It seems obviously unwise to come to conclusions on a person's character from a historical novel in which they are a character, but the dude did lead like 17,000 people to their deaths....<br /><br />In a lot of ways these books seem like the anti-<i>Sharpe</i>. They're both long-running adventure series about a single man's time in the British army in the 19th century. Despite the similarities, I'm fairly comfortable in saying that Sharpe would find Flashman repulsive and vice versa. It's too bad Fraser is no longer living, as how awesome would it be to have a Sharpe/Flashman team-up novel? The answer is objectively, unarguably awesome. I'll certainly read <i>Royal Flash</i> after enjoying this one but I don't know if I could ever binge read this series, purely because it'd be weird to spend that much time with such an ass, hilarious though he is. There were certainly moments in this book where I just thought Flashman was just being a piece of shit, and not in a funny or likable way. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The rape scene...yes he adds that he didn't enjoy it and never did it again but writing a character like this is like walking a tightrope and at that moment Fraser fell the fuck off. I don't consider myself particularly sensitive but I certainly would not enjoy reading more of that. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> An impressive first novel, warts and all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7250822864622482377" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_269638455" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/269638455" />
    <a title="Ensiform" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3843117-ensiform"><img alt="Ensiform" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1275973241p2/3843117.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/269638455?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ensiform" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ensiform" href="/user/show/3843117-ensiform">Ensiform</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3843117-ensiform?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3843117-ensiform?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer269638455" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9695988689974441308">The first of the Flashman Papers, in which our hero joins the army and serves in Afghanistan. This chronicle covers 1839-42: we see Flashy undergoing the pathetic retreat from Kabul under the dithering General Elphinstone, and unwillingly making a last stand at Piper’s Fort.<br/><br/>Although no stranger to Flashy’s exploits, of course, I had never actually read this, the first exploit that cemented his reputation. Here we see a Flashy that is perhaps more cowardly than in the later books; he actually bre</span>
  <span id="freeText9695988689974441308" style="display:none">The first of the Flashman Papers, in which our hero joins the army and serves in Afghanistan. This chronicle covers 1839-42: we see Flashy undergoing the pathetic retreat from Kabul under the dithering General Elphinstone, and unwillingly making a last stand at Piper’s Fort.<br /><br />Although no stranger to Flashy’s exploits, of course, I had never actually read this, the first exploit that cemented his reputation. Here we see a Flashy that is perhaps more cowardly than in the later books; he actually breaks down into hysterics and stops caring about appearances. This can be explained by either Fraser’s growing affection for the character, or (within the fictional framework) Flashy becoming more used to combat situations as he gets older. Flashy is also more of a cad here; he rapes a woman, for one thing, something he never does again. All in all, this is a terrific introduction to the series. All the humor, wit, erudition and historical accuracy of the others books is already present and accounted for. It’s not every day you see an idea of genius (pre-existing fictional anti-hero deftly woven into historical drama) executed with genius from the get-go. A splendid work.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9695988689974441308" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('sAoHvJKKeUwCBBAM3LXeu6VeARyYw7gXLPCtAEOj8JRE148NRxcpW/RqiYxBuQy/r2wqB9RVI9bFwvdL3LD4hg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UIvSRHkHa0NfpLob8bf8lPT3WIVhMbffolTUK8E8NdCkVlr1rJo7VKnKI5tsuy6Q/sVzni2nLB5LZo5gXi89wg==')})
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17027005358269503113">He is best known for his Flashman series of historical novels, purportedly written by Harry Flashman, a fictional coward and bully originally created by Thomas Hughes in Tom Brown's School Days. The novels are presented as "packets" of memoirs written by the nonagenarian Flashman, who looks back on his days as a hero of the British Army during the 19th century. The series begins with Flashman, and</span>
  <span id="freeText17027005358269503113" style="display:none">He is best known for his Flashman series of historical novels, purportedly written by Harry Flashman, a fictional coward and bully originally created by Thomas Hughes in Tom Brown's School Days. The novels are presented as "packets" of memoirs written by the nonagenarian Flashman, who looks back on his days as a hero of the British Army during the 19th century. The series begins with Flashman, and is notable for the accuracy of the historical settings and praise from critics. P.G. Wodehouse said of Flashman, “If ever there was a time when I felt that ‘watcher-of-the-skies-when-a-new-planet’ stuff, it was when I read the first Flashman.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="17027005358269503113" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('bookCover_142463').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('bookCover_142463').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('bookCover_142463').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

</li>
</ul>
</div>

It was a common custom at that time, in the more romantic females, to see their soldier husbands and sweethearts as Greek heroes, instead of the whore-mongering, drunken clowns most of them were.
